B007L96VCY|5.0|I really think a lot of people missed the point of this album when it was released 40 years ago. And hindsight has not helped many others.<br /><br />Ram marked an important step in McCartney's post-Beatles-divorce rehab. If the McCartney solo album marked Paul's first tentative steps back into the studio, Ram marked an important step forward. McCartney retreated, Linda in tow, to hippy life on the farm - \\"lookin' for life in the country\\" -  and re-discovered the simple joys of living outside the bubble.  Life  revolved around family. You see it in the well-know accompanying photographs (some not as well known). This was Paul & Linda's album. While Linda's contributions to Paul's music may still be debatable (god rest her soul) there is no denying that her voice is as deeply embedded in our aural memories of this album as Paul's. Her singing in Admiral Halsey is nothing if not iconic at this point. In Ram, Paul discovered the joys of loosening up, not worrying about perfection, and frankly, not giving a damn what anyone else thoughts about his songs.<br /><br />He also re-discovered his own roots: 3 chord 50s doo-wop based music. We hear this in songs like 3 Legs, Eat At Home, and Smile Away - pure 1950's fluff. It was as far from psychedelic Pepperland as he could get.<br /><br />The songs were, and remain, thrilling pop. Too Many People, explains McCartney's reasons for his return to the farm, as well as the simplicity of the album. It was - and remains - a stunning album opener. Arena crowds were thrilled to hear it live during the 2009(?) tour. (Interestingly, Paul introduced it during the tour as being \\"...for all you Wings fans out there\\". Which was rather odd, as Wings hadn't yet formed.<br /><br />UA/AH remains as timeless, and catchy - impossible to get out of your head really - as it did when it first dominated the AM/FM airwaves. I still see kids - my grandkids - dancing and laughing to the refrain. I mean really - who can resist smiling upon hearing Linda's \\"hands across the water\\"? Monkberry Moon Delight is, well delightful; a raucous, rocking ode to home brew. McCartney takes it a bit over the top at the end, but, that's McCartney. (And just humor me for a moment: imagine, if you will, in your mind's ear - Dylan singing Monkberry Moon Delight with the Travelling Wilburies!)<br /><br />Dear Boy is one of those beautiful seamless melodies that McCartney seems able to lift out of the air. The \\"ba-ba-ba, ba-ba-ba\\" Serendipity Singers pseudo baroque background singing remains perfect. Backseat Of My Car continues to have a simple majesty that is inexplicably timeless, given the simple sentiment expressed in the song. What is ukelele based Ram On, if not the rhythmic precursor to the mandolin based Dance Tonight? And don't even get me started on Smile Away. I'm biased. My early garage band used it to warm up and sound check. Many fond memories there. (\\"A-yai-yai-yai-yai-yai-yai-yai - - \\"). Crank up Smile Away; just listen to the texture in that fuzz bass!<br /><br />The remastering is brilliant; Paul's bass is not nearly as prominent as the Beatles remasters, and that's all for the good. The sound is more cohesive. The bonus disc (which seems to be garnering more than its fair share of attention from those desiring more or less or different, is 34 minutes of Paulie pleasure. I have always been a huge fan of the original Another Day/Woman, Oh Why single and it was great to hear the latter again. Just listen to the flexibility of McCartney's voice - both in WWOW, as well as the opening of Two Many People.<br /><br />Ram continues to delight. It was a brilliant step up from \\"McCartney\\" (excepting Every Night and Maybe I'm Amazed), light ears away from the debacle of Wings' Wild Life, and a hook-laden foundation for McCartney's true shot of redemption - Band On The Run. As a snapshot of the artist during a very transitional period of his life, Ram has nothing to apologize for. It was, in Paul Simon's words, \\"a time of innocence/a time of consequences\\".<br /><br />Indeed. Preserve your memories - they're all that's left you. (And memory's almost full).<br /><br />Godspeed, Sir Paul! (from the guy who met you and Nancy on line for popcorn at the Zeigfeld Theater at the opening weekend of Indiana Jones and the Crystal Skull)cence/a time of consequences\\".<br /><br />Indeed. Preserve your memories - they're all that's left you. (And memory's almost full).<br /><br />Godspeed, Sir Paul! (from the guy who met you and Nancy on line for popcorn at the Zeigfeld Theater at the opening weekend of Indiana Jones and the Crystal Skull)

0307719219|3.0|One of the big questions in economics is why some nations are rich and some are poor.  This is beyond a mere theoretical question because the answers generated effect how we try to provide aid and resources to lift these nations out of poverty.  A current model is that nations are poor because their leaders lack enough information to make sound economical decisions for their country.  If we just give them resources, they will make better decisions and their nations will begin to prosper.  Of course, we've been trying this for decades so if that is the correct answer we should have solved the world's prosperity problems by now. In this book, the author's propose the theory that the type of government a nation has is the primary reason why a nation will either prosper of fail.  If the government is extractive (it exists to extract from its people so that a small group can prosper) the nation will fail.  If the people have the power and the government exists to serve the will of the people, an inclusive government, the nation is much more likely to prosper. Thus, supplying an extractive government with aid and resources will never result in prosperity because the ruling elite will siphon the money/resources off for their own means and ends.<br /><br />There is a great deal of truth that seems inherent to this argument.  However, the authors try to argue that other factors such as geography or culture matter little when it comes to whether a nation will prosper and fail. The authors argue that, contrary to the thesis of Jared Diamond's Gun's Germs and Steel, it is government, not geography that determines a nation's prosperity.  Well, what determines government?  Diamond's thesis argued that geography was critical to government and that the geographical constraints of Europe were critical for the type of culture that arose which gave rise to the type of government that followed.  If it is only government and not other factors such as geography, why is there such a difference in prosperity between places with the same government but where the geography is vastly different?  The Ozarks are rural, mountainous, and lack good soil for crops and the region is very poor.  Yet this is in the US so this should be a prosperous region because its people have access to the same government systems as those in St. Louis, a much more prosperous region which is just over a hundred miles away. Clearly, there is a role for geography in determining prosperity.<br /><br />The authors make interesting arguments but they bend and cherry pick arguments to solely fit their thesis.  The problem with the real world is that the answers are never this easy.  Nations are poor for a variety of reasons, government being only one (although a major one). This was an interesting read but lacks from nuance.  Also, it would have benefited from an editor who could have cut out some of the repetitious sections.rence in prosperity between places with the same government but where the geography is vastly different?  The Ozarks are rural, mountainous, and lack good soil for crops and the region is very poor.  Yet this is in the US so this should be a prosperous region because its people have access to the same government systems as those in St. Louis, a much more prosperous region which is just over a hundred miles away. Clearly, there is a role for geography in determining prosperity.<br /><br />The authors make interesting arguments but they bend and cherry pick arguments to solely fit their thesis.  The problem with the real world is that the answers are never this easy.  Nations are poor for a variety of reasons, government being only one (although a major one). This was an interesting read but lacks from nuance.  Also, it would have benefited from an editor who could have cut out some of the repetitious sections.

Go ahead, say that two, maybe even three films in the series was enough -- but you won't find a lot of viewers who weren't at least mildly entertained by either the first or second entries in the series at least, while parts 3 and 4 made box-office chum of its counterparts financially if nothing else (though with the star profit participation and salaries, I'm not sure the studio made a whole lot of money in the process).<br /><br />After a lengthy delay (the European versions of this set have been available for well over a year), Warner has rolled out a 5-disc LETHAL WEAPON COLLECTION on Blu-Ray, offering improved 1080p transfers of the first two films (previously available only in awful-looking, standalone Blu-Ray platters), the debut of the later sequels, and a bonus documentary. Each film is presented in its original theatrical version with the deleted scenes offered as extras.<br /><br />All the transfers here are quite good, and if you previously owned the old Blu-Ray or HD-DVD editions of the first two films, you'll notice an appreciable improvement in those transfers here, with stronger detail and bolder colors. The third and fourth films fare even better, with DTS MA soundtracks on-hand for each picture.<br /><br />Extra features aren't overwhelming - the main draw are the copious deleted scenes, some of which had been added to prior, director-unapproved \\"Extended Cuts.\\" Donner contributes commentary tracks that are, unfortunately, not all that interesting, dominated by long gaps of silence and with the director having to be prompted by an off-mike moderator at certain points. Music videos are also on-hand throughout the four movie platters, with the fifth disc reserved for a four-part, almost two-hour new documentary. I say \\"new,\\" even though this retrospective talk with the principal players (Donner, Gibson, Glover, etc.) was shot in the spring of 2010. It's an engaging and fun look back at the series' production, showing that the chemistry between the stars and director was, unsurprisingly, as strong off-camera as it was on the screen.<br /><br />Even if the supplements are a bit light, this is still a recommended purchase for \\"Lethal Weapon\\" fans, though the international, region-free box-set release is considerably less expensive (and has the exact same content on it).

The evidence may be complicated, witnesses may be confused or unreliable and the expert opinions may be contradictory or incomprehensible... B004L3AQFG|4.0|The story of Danny Boyle's 127 HOURS is no secret -- it's a true account of a lone hiker who finds himself trapped for days.  This movie has a simple premise, and its not a pleasant one.  Luckily for the audience, the execution here is top notch.  The claustrophobic setting here is conveyed well without ever feeling clunky or confusing.  Danny Boyle, who is known for his highly kinetic shooting and editing, paces this film extraordinarily well.  It's never boring, sluggish, or jarring.  The audience may be trapped with James Franco (who gives the best performance of his career) in this ravine, but the audience is never abused or mistreated.  Additionally, Franco never feels like he is being championed as a hero.  The film gives his character a pretty objective and critical eye; he made mistakes that could have (and should have) been prevented.<br /><br />127 HOURS speaks well of the human condition.  We may look at these circumstances and claim that we could never go to the lengths that Franco goes through to free himself.  The truth is, we would.  The truth is, we need people.  We need to be connected.  The beginning and ending of this film work together extraordinarily well.  In the beginning, society looks crowded, loud, and obtrusive.  By the end, we long for that crowded, loud, and obtrusive society that we thought we wanted to flee from.<br /><br />I'd recommend watching this; it's not easy, but it's rewarding.  Unfortunately, repeat viewings are not sufficiently rewarded, and there isn't much to gain from the Blu-ray version of this either.  For these reasons, I'd suggesting renting this one off Amazon instead of buying it.

B007N3A2F0|3.0|Like any Sonata Arctica fan, I was simply ecstatic when I got an e-mail about the impending release of Stones Grow Her Name. I loved Unia and The Days of Grays to the point where I almost got sick of them but thankfully stopped a little short of reaching such a point. However, in my bliss had almost forgotten an important lesson, a lesson that Unia taught me years earlier.<br /><br />See, when Unia was released I was eager as any fan. I couldn't wait to hear some new Sonata Arctica tunes. But, like some others, I wasn't really looking to hear a *new* Sonata Arctica. So as hasty as it sounds I quickly wrote the album off and went along my way. A year or so later - for reasons I'm not so sure of - I decided to give it another listen and was, quite literally, hit upside the head by what I had been missing. I'm not exactly sure what changed in that year but after that point an odd thing started to happen - a really odd thing. Unlike most people who have no problem getting into an album the first time they hear it my ability to do that ceased to exist. The first thing I hear when listening to a new album now is a mindless mass of instrumentation. It's only after listening to it a couple dozen times that everything comes together and actually works.<br /><br />So what does this have to do with Sonata Arctica? Well everything because when The Days of Grays came around I was wiser and a lot more patient. I pretty much expected the album to sound like a mess and I was right. Only after I really listened to it did everything make sense and finally click. Well, long story short the Stones Grow Her Name falls into the exact same category and well, it seems that Kakko's going to make me work for it. Maybe the feeling will disappear with time but this album is an odd creature to say the least. Odd as it may be, I'm not sure if I can detect the prize underneath the facade but there are some moments that are immediately accessible. The opening number will make one feel more than welcome and \\"I Have a Right\\" works (and is a lot less corny) than the lyrics would have you believe.<br /><br />However, there are a few things that crop up that are a bit troubling. The first, which may sound a little silly, is the over-dependence of song titles in the songs themselves. This really stands out because in previous albums Kakko really shied away from this and would only quote the title once or twice throughout the entire song. Using the title over and over in certain songs (\\"Losing My Insanity\\") takes away from the complexity of the lyrics which Kakko is a known master at. In all seriousness, \\"The Dead Skin\\" on The Days of Grays proves that alone. The other problem is what Kakko turns the Wildfire series into - pro earth propaganda. I'll be the first to admit I'm not really a \\"save the earth\\" kind of guy but I had enough patience for and even liked \\"Respect the Wilderness\\" and \\"Wrecking the Sphere\\" (and I even liked how they tastefully dabbled in politics and war with \\"Zeros\\") but this is really pushing it. Did the original Wildfire on Reckoning Night have anything to do with the environment? I thought it was more about a man scorned.<br /><br />So anyway take the three stars with a gain of salt - in a week this could be my favorite album. Still, I have a feeling that there is something unseen that will prohibit from reaching the heights Unia and the Days of Grays have obtained in my personally hierarchy. That aside, perhaps the greatest thing I can say about Stones Grow her name is it is another in a long list of albums that challenges the listener and you can't put a price on that.

B0081X40HW|5.0|Haley is a smart, talented girl blessed with a unique voice. We're blessed because she also has the desire to create \\"good\\" music.<br /><br />Her parents have been working musicians for more than three decades so she grew up in a home where a variety of music was played, discussed and explored. During her run on Idol, this was evident in that she more than capably performed music from a variety of genres and time periods (from Adele to Zep).<br /><br />Until she did a stunning duet on the show, no one knew her true love was Jazz. It's easy to understand its allure to her. Jazz is dynamic musically and values improvisation, which makes it the best genre for someone with an instrument as versatile as Haley's (she did an impromptu performance at Irvin Mayfield's Jazz club in New Orleans and he was so impressed he invited her to perform with him at Carnegie Hall this October).<br /><br />\\"Listen up!\\" is not a Jazz album but it incorporates Jazz elements. There's a dynamic element to most of the songs as she vocally plays with different words and lines. Those lines include some wonderful lyrics that Haley had a hand in writing.<br /><br />When Haley was first signed, she stated in an interview she would likely have to \\"fight her way in\\" and make it clear the type of album she wanted to make and that she wanted to be hands on in its creation. Turns out they were more than willing to work with her and the album turned out great as a result.<br /><br />In the words of the New York Times... \\"these songs hark back to the 1960s and '70s, when Motown girl groups were ceding ground to soul-driven rock. It's singers' territory that shows off both the smooth power of Ms. Reinhart's voice and its engine-revving churn...In contemporary pop terms, this is territory staked out by the likes of Bruno Mars, and the long shadow of Amy Winehouse hangs over the more wrenching soul numbers...On \\"Hit the Ground Runnin',\\" Ms. Reinhart easily channels the Supremes, blending melody and sass...It's unfashionable and a bit out of touch, and what a smart thing that turns out to be.\\"
B00846WWVM|5.0|I've seen &#34;Sprinkle&#34; compared to &#34;Angry Birds,&#34; but it reminded me more of &#34;Where's My Water,&#34; since it requires directing a quantity of water to certain locations, although beyond that, there's not much in common. With Sprinkle, you're using an adjustable, aim-able water cannon to put out fires before they burn down the villagers' houses.<br /><br />Sprinkle is a fast-paced game, and you have to be quick. On each level, the action begins immediately. For me, it's a two-handed game, with my right thumb pushing the water button, and my left thumb controlling the height and direction of the water cannon. For some levels, you can hose down the fires directly, but on most levels, you'll have to use different strategies; for example, spraying a rock to roll it into position to act as a dam.<br /><br />The game is very challenging, and it's going to take me many hours to beat every level. At least if you're stuck on a level, you can move on to the next level and come back later. Your score depends on how much water you have to use to put out all the fires. That makes the replay value of Sprinkle very high, since you always have the challenge of improving your score.<br /><br />My only (slightly) negative comment is about the way you aim the water cannon. You'll have to experiment a bit to coordinate moving the water cannon up and down and to aim it. It's tricky until you get the hang of it.<br /><br />I'm playing Sprinkle on a Kindle Fire. The cartoon-like graphics are excellent, and the game plays smoothly with no hesitations. It's a good game for anyone who wants a real challenge.
1455518999|4.0|SETUP:  Army CID John Puller is assigned to look into the death of an army officer in a West Virginia coal mining town.  He is assisted by local police sargeant Samantha Cole.  Of course, a romatic relationship develops.<br /><br />EVALUATION:  \\"Zero Day\\" is a well told fast-paced story, containing engaging characters.  It is similar to earlier Baldacci novels (such as the early Maxwell & King and early Camel Club).  The build up to the \\"mystery\\" was terrific.  The \\"discovery\\" was disappointing---Puller was just informed, and even that seem a bit far-fetched.  After that point the plot rambled rather aimlessly.  The climax was bogus---real bombs do not have clock displays nor tricky booby traps, and no-one is going to roll 55-gallon drums of uranium (which would weigh maybe 10 tons each) into a mine shaft.  Besides there is absolutely no reason for a shaft to exist within the building.  Nothing after Samantha's death really added anything to the story.  Readers and reviewers would have howled if the story had been cut off there, but doing so might have been the better choice. The ending, more of an \\"afterward\\" was very disappointing---maybe I just lost interest.  The unnecessarily complex, poorly told, backstory of how the bad guys are and their motivations is garbled, farfetched, and presented without an inadequate foundation.  If after reading the last page, you scratched your head and said \\"Huh?\\"---it's Baldacci's fault, not yours. Even so, I was hooked before the plot begins to deteriorate, and willing to grant Baldacci as much \\"suspension of disbelief\\" as he needed.  Overall, a thoroughly enjoyable read. Not a terrific read, but a good one.<br /><br />INACCURACIES:  As a published natural resources historian, with relatives who live in one of those West Virginia coal towns, whom I visit every year, I was dismayed by Baldacci's inaccuracies.<br /><br />Mining:  This is the second novel Baldacci has partly set in a mine, and he still doesn't know the meaning of the word \\"shaft\\".  Not to meantion that there are relatively few shafts in West Virginia---most underground coal mines are entered by \\"drifts\\".  There are problems associated with coal mining.  The environmental problems are pretty much the same as would be associated with any large scale construction, such as building a highway, suburb or an airport.  (And indeed there are far more acres of land \\"disturbed\\" by airport construction in the US than disturbed by mining).  Yes, water tables are often disrupted (by highway building, construction, or mining) and natural gas can sometimes leak into aquifers.  So?  Build a water treatment plant and install water lines---that's what's done elsewhere.  The sewage from outhouses or septic tanks does more real damage to the water supply than mining.  The health risks are mostly limited to underground miners, but are substantial.  But as Baldacci points out, most mining is now from much safer open cut methods, which is no more dangerous than highway or building construction.  I know of no increases in cancer in WV coal mining areas.  Little coal dust gets into the air (even in surface mines, the dirt is sprayed with water to keep dust down).  Acid rain used to happen where you burn coal, NOT where you mine it.  With current technology very little sulfur gets into the air even from coal-fired electrical power plants.<br /><br />Social Problems:  There ARE social problems in WV coal mining towns, but not those portrayed by Baldacci.  The main problems are slougth and ignorance.  The main cause is actually the \\"free money\\"---the relatively enormous salaries paid to coal miners for relatively unskilled labor.  Why bother going to High-School when you can earn $50,000 a year as an apprentice in a coal mine?  Okay, the work (underground mining) is backbreaking and dangerous---try telling that to a 16 year-old-kid with a pregnant girlfriend.  Taxes and insurance are virtually non-existant compared to urban America.  Housing is ridiculously inexpensive.  The only utilities most rural West Virginians pay for are electricity and cable TV.  $50,000 in rural WV is the equivalent of $100,000 per year in an average size American city, $200,000 in SF, LA, or NYC. With minimal skill (e.g., an electrician's license) the salary for underground work is upward of $100,000.  Or, you can work one year and then permanently \\"retire\\" with a \\"bad back\\" on various government benefits and programs which add up to more than $50,000/yr.  Although undermotivated, most small town West Virginians are true salt-of-the-earth people---good solid Americans who try to live decent lives.  These are NOT the despirately poor with no options.<br /><br />Finally, while I have no doubt that the US Government is capable of simply abandoning and forgetting hazzardous facilities, even the United States Government is not stupid enough to build a nuclear enrichment reactor in a coal mining area in West Virginia.  Probably not.  Maybe not.  Well, maybe.<br /><br />COMPARISON TO LEE CHILDS:  Every voracious reader and writer (I am both) knows that there is alot of cross-over between authors, which is less than plagerism.  Very often an author doesn't know where he gets his ideas from.  I haven't read the Jack Reacher novels, but now I plan to.  Caveat, I found Child's \\"Deep Storm\\" and \\"Terminal Freeze\\" to be maybe B-; good action, but with serious flaws.<br /><br />COMPARISON TO \\"THE GENERAL'S DAUGHTER\\":  I was actually immediately reminded of Nelson Demille's \\"The General's Daughter\\".  The main characters (Paul Brenner and Cynthia Sunhill) are very similar (even their names are similar) to John Puller and Symantha Cole.  Both Breener and Puller are CID Warrant Officers.  Albeit, I must admit that \\"The General's Daughter\\" was MUCH better---gripping to the very last page.  I guess that dates me as a geezer---since no other reviews mention the similarity of characters.sly inexpensive. B007L96VCY|4.0|RAM was originally released in May 1971, and it really needs to be put into context to understand the critical reaction. The Beatles had broken up a little over a year prior, and since Paul broke the news to the world, most people considered the breakup his fault. On top of that, his first solo album, MCCARTNEY, was a pleasant listen but was a very homemade affair. He recorded it all by himself, with a little help from Linda on some backing vocals, and most of the songs are nice acoustic ditties which sound as if they're not quite finished. Coming off of the Beatles' polished masterpiece ABBEY ROAD, it was more than a bit of a disappointment.<br /><br />So Paul decided to do his next record up right. He auditioned a number of respected session players before selecting the talented Denny Seiwell on drums and David Spinozza on guitar. They recorded over several months in New York and L.A., with the New York Philharmonic providing orchestration on several tracks. Spinozza played on several songs, but was then fired since he didn't want to be at McCartney's beck and call, and Hugh McCracken replaced him. (Spinozza went on to play guitar for Yoko Ono after she threw John out, and McCracken ended up playing on DOUBLE FANTASY.)<br /><br />\\"Another Day\\", backed with \\"Oh Woman Oh Why\\", was the advance single, which didn't end up on the album. At the time, it was slammed for being a lightweight version of \\"Eleanor Rigby\\", with its story about a secretary and her drab and lonely life. I didn't hear the song until years later, when I bought WINGS GREATEST, and I mistook it for a Wings song for years. Either way, it's a beautiful ballad with lovely harmonies and some truly gorgeous slide guitar from Spinozza. I think the song was meant to convey a feeling, which the beginning of the chorus sums up quite well:<br /><br />So sad, so sad.<br />Sometimes she feels so sad.<br /><br />\\"Oh Woman Oh Why\\" is completely different and even better, a ripping rocker in which Paul unleashes his best screaming vocals. It's hard to understand many of the vocals, but that's what Google is for. I found them rather intriguing and strangely kinky. So no idea what Paul was thinking, but it's a keeper of a song.<br /><br />RAM itself has a sound unlike any record Paul's done since, and that sound is epic pop. Sonically, the record has a lot of depth, with echo on some of the vocals and instruments, but with a clarity and preciseness that keeps it from being mushy. Paul has never sung better than on this record; he handles slow ballads and all-out rockers with equal ease. The arrangements are simply gorgeous, with Linda's harmonies, for the most part, meshing with Paul's own to create some luscious backing vocals. It's obvious to me that Eric Carmen and his band, the Raspberries, took a great deal of inspiration from RAM.<br /><br />Still, the critics hated RAM just as much, if not more, than MCCARTNEY. Jon Landau, the Rolling Stone reviewer, called it \\"the nadir in the decomposition of Sixties rock thus far.\\" (Rolling Stone has more recently revised its opinion upward.) Robert Christgau was equally unkind, and British reviewers Roy Carr and Tony Tyler called it \\"stay-prest, ready-to-wear music, the kind to be listened to in the living room with ducks on the wall.\\" Sheesh. Why all the vitrol for a record with pop as gorgeous as this?<br /><br />A big part of the reason was that it *was* pop, rather than rock music. John Lennon had just come out with a Serious Rock Record (which is excellent), PLASTIC ONO BAND. George Harrison had wowed critics and fans with his Phil Spector-produced Serious Masterpiece, ALL THINGS MUST PASS. RAM, in comparison, seemed trivial. Lightweight. Unimportant. That factor of \\"Paul-broke-up-the-Beatles\\" also came into play, as did many of the lyrical references. More on that in a minute.<br /><br />For myself, I came to RAM in 1978 or '79, long after the critics had moved on. I knew their opinions, and I knew that I loved the single fromthe album, \\"Uncle Albert/Admiral Halsey\\". So I hoped for some music as great as the single, and for the most part, I got it. Those lyrics, though....<br /><br />Let's go through RAM song by song, so you get an idea of how it just misses perfection with those silly words.<br /><br />\\"Too Many People\\"--A nice, midpaced rocker with gruff vocals from Paul and Linda's harmonies at key points. It slows down in the chorus to give the song a pensive feel, and the riffs from McCracken are fantastic. There's that echo on the voice and guitar. Nice drumming, too. But when Paul goes on about \\"too many people preaching practices\\" and \\"That was your first mistake...You took your lucky break and broke it in two/Now what can be done for you?/You broke it in two.\\", the impression that he's singing about John Lennon cannot be escaped, even now. (Paul admitted later that yes, he was.) That starts things off on an uncomfortable note. It just gets worse.<br /><br />\\"3 Legs\\"--Great bluesy acoustic guitar picking and nice vocals, although Linda sounds rather nasal. But here we are with \\"My dog he got three legs/but he can't run...When I thought you was my friend/But you let me down, put my heart around the bend.\\" 3 Legs. Three ex-Beatles. Get it? Maybe just coincidence, but that same mean-spiritedness even if so. (Spinozza takes lead here and on \\"Eat At Home\\"; otherwise, the rest of the album is McCracken.)<br /><br />\\"Ram On\\"--A fragment of a ditty on ukelele, with sweet singing from Paul. No lyrical allusions here (except that \\"Ram On\\" is Paul's code for \\"Ramone\\" which was his pseudonym back when the Beatles were starting out [and the Ramones got their name from that]), and some gorgeous backing oohs. Whistling and random piano chords and studio talk. Very cute, although yeah, lightweight.<br /><br />\\"Dear Boy\\"--Lovely, tuneful pop song, with those gorgeous oohs and ahs from Linda (Paul helped her a lot). But here we are again. \\"Hope you never know, dear boy, how much you missed\\"? John thought that was about him. In actual fact, it was about Linda's ex-husband, which makes more sense, but I can see how John thought it was aimed in his direction. Again, lots of little digs at people. I think Paul was feeling defensive around then.<br /><br />\\"Uncle Albert/Admiral Halsey\\"--I thought this was the Beatles when I first heard it. I could have sworn it was. It's like son of \\"Penny Lane\\" or \\"Hello Goodbye\\". Absolutely lovely production, with McCracken's riffs sounding just like George Harrison. Changes musical direction four or five times. Excellent. Still, \\"We're so sorry if we caused you any pain?\\" Maybe aimed at the other three. Maybe. I personally always wondered if Paul was inspired by the Uncle Albert in MARY POPPINS, who came down from the ceiling when he cried. Who knows.<br /><br />\\"Smile Away\\"--What a neat rocker. Even with Linda's annoying \\"Don't know how to do that\\" nasally going on over and over, it still moves right along. More screaming from Paul. I gotta believe this is aimed at the other three, though, what with \\"I met a friend of mine and he did say/Man, I can smell your feet a mile away/Smile away!\\" All this lyrical attacking, whether it be Beatles or folks in general, really gets old.<br /><br />Now we're onto Side Two, back in the good old vinyl days. Here we start on the \\"home, family, love\\" songs.<br /><br />\\"Heart of the Country\\"--Super acoustic guitar picking, and a nice vocal. But anyone singing \\"I want a horse, I want a sheep\\" better be prepared to be laughed at. I didn't like the scat singing on the guitar breaks, either, but I appreciate it more now.<br /><br />\\"Monkberry Moon Delight\\"--The song that annoys me most. It's a super piano-based rocker, with absolutely tonsil-shredding singing from Paul, a la \\"Helter Skelter\\". Fantastic. But those words make absolutely *no sense at all*. \\"So I sat in the attic, a piano up my nose\\"? \\"Catch up, soup and puree, don't get left behind\\"? \\"When I leave my pajamas to Billy Budapest\\"? Thisisn't even good pseudo-Dylan. It's random phrases that don't add up to anything coherent. It's not even funny, just dumb. Apparently it's Paul's ode to getting high. Well, *duh*. Wish he'd tried harder and came up with a real classic. Oh, and I don't much care for Linda's vocals here. In the early days, she sang *everything* through her nose.<br /><br />\\"Eat At Home\\"--I had no clue what this song was really about until I heard it discussed on a Beatles podcast. Add it to the list of great McCartney double-entendre lyrics. At any rate, it's a wonderful Buddy Holly-style rocker, especially on the middle eight. The joys of home cooking.<br /><br />\\"Long Haired Lady\\"--An ode to the lovely Linda. Beautiful orchestration, and it really takes off when it gets to the \\"Ah, love is long, love is long\\" part of the song. Again, I don't like the nasalness of Linda's contribution: \\"Or is this the only thing you want me *for*...\\" (shudder) You've still got that lovely echoing guitar, though, with great riffs and super drumming. It fades into a reprise of \\"Ram On\\", which ends with a cute bit of the future song \\"Big Barn Bed\\", which showed up on the next album. Nice of Paul to give us a teaser.<br /><br />\\"Back Seat of My Car\\"--The Brian Wilson production number, complete with car-as-place-to-have-sex metaphor. It's great. This must have been the inspiration for the Raspberries' \\"Go All The Way\\". Strings and harmonies and horns and glory, and wonder of wonders, no mean-spiritedness or celebrations of domestic bliss. Just \\"We believe that we can't be wrong.\\" Ends the album on a high note.<br /><br />I still love RAM. I think it's a fantastic record, just as it is. But I can never listen to it without wanting on some level to slap Paul for all the lyrical digs and fatuousness. So while it's a favorite album, it's not one of his best. Well worth a listen, though.<br /><br />Oh, and let's not forget the cutesy-pie collage of photos of Paul & Linda on the inside and the photo of the beetle screwing another beetle on the back. Yeah, no secret reference there.<br /><br />The bonus tracks aren't bad, although they're mostly session jams and instrumentals. \\"Little Woman Love\\" is fantastic, with Henry McCullough overdubbing guitar; it was originally the B-side of \\"Mary Had A Little Lamb\\", which will probably show up on WILD LIFE. \\"A Love For You\\" is not a bad tune, and has a great tonsil-shredding vocal from Paul. \\"Hey Diddle\\" is a sweet little acoustic duet between Paul & Linda. Still don't like her voice, but to be fair, she improved greatly over the years as a backing vocalist.<br /><br />As far as the packaging: The glue on my copy has come loose from Disc 1's side, so it's having a hard time staying in. The bonus photos are sweet, and it's nice to have the lyrics at long last, as witness to Linda's backing vocal on \\"Smile Away\\". [I never knew that was what she was singing until I read the lyric sheet.] Wish that they'd identified the original musicians on each track, though.<br /><br />Final thought: When I first bought RAM, I considered it underrated, since most people didn't like it. Nowadays I consider it overrated.thought that was about him. In actual fact, it was about Linda's ex-husband, which makes more sense, but I can see how John thought it was aimed in his direction. Again, lots of little digs at people. I think Paul was feeling defensive around then.<br /><br />\\"Uncle Albert/Admiral Halsey\\"--I thought this was the Beatles when I first heard it. I could have sworn it was. It's like son of \\"Penny Lane\\" or \\"Hello Goodbye\\". Absolutely lovely production, with McCracken's riffs sounding just like George Harrison. Changes musical direction four or five times. Excellent. Still, \\"We're so sorry if we caused you any pain?\\" Maybe aimed at the other three. Maybe. I personally always wondered if Paul was inspired by the Uncle Albert in MARY POPPINS, who came down from the ceiling when he cried. Who knows.<br /><br />\\"Smile Away\\"--What a neat rocker. Even with Linda's annoying \\"Don't know how to do that\\" nasally going on over and over, it still moves right along. More screaming from Paul. I gotta believe this is aimed at the other three, though, what with \\"I met a friend of mine and he did say/Man, I can smell your feet a mile away/Smile away!\\" All this lyrical attacking, whether it be Beatles or folks in general, really gets old.<br /><br />Now we're onto Side Two, back in the good old vinyl days. Here we start on the \\"home, family, love\\" songs.<br /><br />\\"Heart of the Country\\"--Super acoustic guitar picking, and a nice vocal. But anyone singing \\"I want a horse, I want a sheep\\" better be prepared to be laughed at. I didn't like the scat singing on the guitar breaks, either, but I appreciate it more now.<br /><br />\\"Monkberry Moon Delight\\"--The song that annoys me most. It's a super piano-based rocker, with absolutely tonsil-shredding singing from Paul, a la \\"Helter Skelter\\". Fantastic. But those words make absolutely *no sense at all*. \\"So I sat in the attic, a piano up my nose\\"? \\"Catch up, soup and puree, don't get left behind\\"? \\"When I leave my pajamas to Billy Budapest\\"? Thisisn't even good pseudo-Dylan. It's random phrases that don't add up to anything coherent. It's not even funny, just dumb. Apparently it's Paul's ode to getting high. Well, *duh*. Wish he'd tried harder and came up with a real classic. Oh, and I don't much care for Linda's vocals here. In the early days, she sang *everything* through her nose.<br /><br />\\"Eat At Home\\"--I had no clue what this song was really about until I heard it discussed on a Beatles podcast. Add it to the list of great McCartney double-entendre lyrics. At any rate, it's a wonderful Buddy Holly-style rocker, especially on the middle eight. The joys of home cooking.<br /><br />\\"Long Haired Lady\\"--An ode to the lovely Linda. Beautiful orchestration, and it really takes off when it gets to the \\"Ah, love is long, love is long\\" part of the song. Again, I don't like the nasalness of Linda's contribution: \\"Or is this the only thing you want me *for*...\\" (shudder) You've still got that lovely echoing guitar, though, with great riffs and super drumming. It fades into a reprise of \\"Ram On\\", which ends with a cute bit of the future song \\"Big Barn Bed\\", which showed up on the next album. Well worth a listen, though.<br /><br />Oh, and let's not forget the cutesy-pie collage of photos of Paul & Linda on the inside and the photo of the beetle screwing another beetle on the back. Yeah, no secret reference there.<br /><br />The bonus tracks aren't bad, although they're mostly session jams and instrumentals. \\"Little Woman Love\\" is fantastic, with Henry McCullough overdubbing guitar; it was originally the B-side of \\"Mary Had A Little Lamb\\", which will probably show up on WILD LIFE. \\"A Love For You\\" is not a bad tune, and has a great tonsil-shredding vocal from Paul. \\"Hey Diddle\\" is a sweet little acoustic duet between Paul & Linda. B007MAETA8|4.0|The great (and at times, confusing for many) thing about John Mayer is that you can't simply pinpoint his genre. An acoustic love ballad pop artist? I invite you to listen to Heavier Things, a swinging, melodic mashup of contemporary rock and emotional prowess. Or perhaps Continuum (my personal favorite) a flowing complication of influences from his idols into a unique, contemporary and thought invoking blues paragon. His last album, Battle Studies, dove into a cheery blend of acousto-pop and rock. Now, we see this evolution continue in his newest studio release, Born And Raised. Highly influenced and intended to be of a country-esque vibe, perhaps, even portrayed as music written by nomadic cowboys under the stars. (The previous is based off statements made by John Mayer himself.) As far as lyrics go, this is honest, down-to-earth, perhaps even personal reflections. This is honestly as revealing as an artist can get without revealing the gritty details. A self-preservation of life through their eyes in a Hemingway type of Iceberg theory. Listen to the lyrics, and connect the dots, and see how it plays out. The lyrics, while sappy at times, can be very conjuring of thoughts: Take for example the song \\"Love is a Verb.\\" A personal showing on relationships and the need for action on both ends, and the anti-movement of simply flaunting a thing such as love as a mere material possession. In the historically based \\"Walt Grace's Submarine Test, January 1967,\\" we are narrated a story of Mr. Walt Grace and his aspirations to deep sea exploration despite the objection of those who doubt him. It lends to a sense of need of self belief and struggles with confidence and grooming oneself into what they desire. The same vibe can be induced through ballads of \\"Born and Raised\\" and \\"Born and Raised (Reprise.)\\" A sense of self struggle and perseverance in the song \\"Whiskey Whiskey Whiskey.\\" The lyrics, are at times, somewhat sappy, but never on the verge of full-out \\"cliche'\\" and forsure the most honest of Mr. Mayer's discography. His vocals maintain that unique raspy characteristic and are followed by background echoes from other singers for accentuation and many simultaneous melodic aspects throughout the entire album, signifying the very hometown feel. Musically, we see a John Mayer that is fairly familiar, utilizing many Acoustic finger techniques and arpeggios (noted with \\"If I Ever Get Around to Living\\") However, there are a multitude of songs that accompany John's signature Stratocaster sound (which receives raves constantly from the guitar playing community.) However, it is not the clean, warm, deep sounds of Continuum, which acted as a staple for the album. Instead, it is actually very layered, and at times drowned in reverb and other effects, but never truly sounds overproduced or artificial in such cases. The tone works, and the tone is good. Overall, this phase of John Mayer's musical career may have come rather unexpected, but in all honesty, a move of such drastic degree was predicted by many, but in what direction was subjective. This feel good country influenced sound is a breath of fresh air in any department, and it works, outstandingly well. The lyrics can at times fall and sound corny, but it's honest... and you know what? It sounds good... real good. A 4.5/5 if I could, and I honestly believe it was one of his best albums. (Personal ranking: 1) Continuum 2)Born and Raised 3) Heavier Things)

Tea parties, ice skating, and society life still continue in the Legation Quarter at the time of this story. It is bordered by the Badlands, a place of crime and degradation, the mysterious Fox Tower, as well as all of Peiking, rapidly increasing in population with those who are escaping from life elsewhere. Outside the Legation Quarter many are white Russians whose identity papers are meaningless.  For them this is the end of the line, not only because of useless documents but also destitution. Starving Russian men wander about in tattered Tartar uniforms, soliders from an army that no longer exists. Many of the unfortunate women end up as drug-addicted prostitutes.  Chinese are fleeing the war-torn country side in the north for the tenuous safety of Peiking.<br /><br />Fox Tower is abandoned at night because of a belief that Fox spirits inhabited the tower and would come out at night, dig up graves,and balance skulls on their heads.  If the skulls stayed on their heads when the Fox spirits bowed to the Goddess of the North Star, then they would live for 10 centuries and seek out innocent mortals to steal their energy life force.  That is the one part of the story that to me seemed preposterous although it appears to have been a belief among the Chinese. Who would believe such a ridiculous thing in the twentieth century?  Ghosts, even energy sucking ghosts I can understand, but fox spirits literally digging up graves and putting skulls on their heads?<br /><br />The photo in the book of Fox Tower today shows it as a well-maintained building, likely a tourist feature, while the 1930s photos in the book reveal a structure that looks much more formidable, it's unglazed windows dark openings into an uninviting place.  The ditch next to it has shallow water and muddy banks upon which stands a gaggle of geese. It separated the tower and the well-appointed Legation Quarter where the young victim and her father lived.<br /><br />As some of the other reviewers have mentioned, the author spends quite a bit of text describing the layout of the area, its streets, and buildings. B007L96SI6|5.0|Ah, American Idol debut albums. Dangerous territory. All too often, it seems like talented young singers get produced to death and wind up creating a product that feels neither authentic nor particularly interesting, as if the producers were trying way too hard to turn a singer into a commodity.<br /><br />It's not to say that Haley Reinhart's debut CD is under-produced, exactly... all one would have to do is listen to a few bars of \\"Oh My!\\" to realize that lack of production will not be an issue here. But what sets LISTEN UP! apart is that it feels like we're getting an authentic sense of who this singer is.<br /><br />Haley is a throwback. She sounds like she would have been in perfect company 40 years ago, and her nods to 60s girl groups and 70s Motown are just a lot of fun.<br /><br />Haley's smoky, raspy voice is in fine form throughout. If Randy Jackson hadn't hated her last season, he would have said she could \\"sing the phone book.\\" Of course, no one uses phone books anymore, but I digress. She sounds incredible, especially on \\"Undone\\" and \\"Now That You're Here,\\" which has a refrain that sounds like a jazzy take on a classic Michael Jackson hook.<br /><br />One thing that watchers of American Idol may not have known last season (I surely didn't) is that Ms. Reinhart is a strong songwriter. She co-wrote nine of the ten tracks here, and you'll find hooks galore and some really nice and memorable turns of phrase. \\"Liar\\" is an intriguing take on a relationship that is on the rocks because her man had the nerve to tell her the truth. I haven't heard that one before. Interesting. \\"Hit The Ground Running\\" is what we might have called \\"cheeky\\" back in the day. Haley's nobody's fool, and she lets you know it in that one, with lines like, \\"You better watch your mouth  boy/'cause I don't miss a beat.\\"<br /><br />There's really not a bad cut on this terrific CD. From the opening bars of \\"Oh My,\\" which sounds like a combination of a steamy August night in New Orleans andsomething pulled from a James Bond film from the 70s, to the gorgeous \\"Walking on Heaven,\\" which may be the best Mariah Carey song not sung my Miss Mariah, there is no filler here. Just one great melody after the next.<br /><br />\\"Oh My\\" features a rap by B.o.B. which fits the song well and is clearly Interscope's attempt to create some buzz for an artist who surely deserves it. To me, this is the song of the summer of 2012. It's an absolute blast to blare this as I drive down the highway.<br /><br />The lead single, \\"Free,\\" is a terrific mid-tempo track that showcases Haley's voice nicely. That said, it's also the only song she did not have a hand in writing, and it's the only track that lacks a strong hook, making it a peculiar choice for a first single. Better perhaps might have been \\"Wasted Tears\\" or \\"Hit the Ground Running,\\" which sound like updated takes on The Supremes, or \\"Undone,\\" a stunning ballad that is reminiscent of \\"Someone Like You\\" by Adele.<br /><br />If you like good pop music, you're going to love this CD. I promise. After about five listens, I have a feeling this will be in heavy rotation on my iPhone for months and months to come.

Josh Hartnett, Eric Bana, Sam Shepard, Tom Sizemore, Ewan McGregor, William Fichtner, Jason Isaacs, Orlando Bloom, George Harris, Ewen Bremner are just some of the impressive cast members. The film focuses on the 1993 raid into Mogadishu by the U.S. military to capture Mohamed Farrah Aidid and the complications of this military operation and the incredible intense battle that followed.<br /><br />It often seems that moments of courage are a result of military expedience predilected by military ineffectiveness due to underestimated enemy strength and numbers and unexpected occurrences that put combatants face to face with overwhelming odds. How a general could ever let his men enter a maze-like city surrounded by thousands of hostile inhabitants is not entirely beyond comprehension. What you don't know or entirely understand or underestimate can hurt you. Sam Shepard as that general had to exceed the scope of the original mission and his command putting his men in harm's way in broad daylight. Simply, the initial mission as planned did not end up being cut and dry.<br /><br />Ridley Scott seems uniquely capable of ferreting out the military decisions that led to this debacle but places no blame. However, all the blame is up there on the screen if you choose to take note similar to the way Sam Shepard watches it on his TV monitor. Scott instead places the viewer into a 180-degree maelstrom of bullets, bombs, shrapnel, blood, sweat and tears. In 160 years of technology it is amazing how some of the images are reminiscent of the last stand at the Alamo. What has been learned in all those years? Every good war film needs a hero and Tom Sizemore gives the best performance as one hardened and dedicated officer. The most interesting shot in this film is where a tired and weary group of Americans finally emerge from the city covered in dirt, gunpowder, blood and grease and they are greeted by foreign United Nations troops wearing turbans. These UN fellows offer the Americans glasses of water served from silver trays with white linen napkins draped over their arms. So much for modern technology it would seem.<br /><br />This film is full of heroics of necessity and raw determination. It does not glorify war, but it does give dignity and recognition to those who go in harm's way to preserve our freedoms and ideals. A lot of the camera movement and Hans Zimmer's score emphasize this recognition to everyone involved at those right in the line of fire. 1594202842|3.0|So, first the fascinating and inspiring:  here's a peek inside what I'm sure just about anyone is at least a little curious about: what kind of parenting results in high achievers-- and, putting aside whatever truths or non-truths are in the stereotypes, why so many Asian students are in that category.  I like Chua's unapologetic and direct (just like her practice of Chinese parenting)recounting of her experience with her daughters.  It is quite brave to put this book to a culture that includes things Chua insists are not found in traditional Chinese parenting:  the helicopter parents, the parents who want to be \\"friends\\" with their kids. And it wouldn't hurt to set down whatever shock or insult one may have in reading this, and consider taking a page or two from her book.  Why not expect a little more from kids (and yourself?)  Why not shoot for excellence, for the A? Why not consider that there are many social problems in the West whose roots might be found in a cultural style that bleeds into parenting?<br /><br />Now the \\"eh.\\"  I don't think Chua gives fair acknowledgement to the drawbacks of her parenting style, and the value to be found in the \\"Western\\" style.  Chua regularly adopts a mocking tone toward the West that does not seem at all tongue-in-cheek.  She obviously has a sense of superiority and makes no apologies for it.  But you have to question why her parents would have come to the U.S. if not for what it had to offer them that they could not have had in China.  I do not expect Chua's book to be any homage to the U.S. But here is a  woman who has reaped so many advantages from it, yet she has not examined what it is about the U.S. that keeps her--with her daughters--in the U.S.  That is a pretty glaring contradiction that she does not attend to.  I'll give her that Western culture is far from perfect.  But she didn't write this book in China.  So maybe Western parents are doing something right.  Let's give some acknowledgement where it's due.  Otherwise, this is all just hubris.

B000002NJS|5.0|This is hands-down the best Madonna album (which is saying something, because there are a lot of great ones) and my favorite album of all time. Madonna + William Orbit = Brilliance, simply put. I would encourage anyone who likes this CD to check out William Orbit's album \\"Pieces in a Modern Style 2\\", his ambient/trance versions of a bunch of classical pieces.<br /><br />\\"Drowned World/Substitute for Love\\" - A chilled-out work of excellence. Madonna's newly trained, expressive voice is well on display, and it works perfectly with Orbit's bubblingly ambient, underwater soundscape.<br /><br />\\"Swim\\" - Madonna turns into John Lennon and starts singing about healing the world. A groovy, breakbeat-fueled jam with a surprisingly catchy melody with some fascinating guitar work.<br /><br />\\"Ray of Light\\" - an ecstatic masterpiece. Orbit's electrifying track soars with Madonna's amazing voice. I don't think I need to say anymore, as this is one of Madonna's best-known and most-acclaimed singles.<br /><br />\\"Candy Perfume Girl\\" - plays a lot of similar notes to \\"Swim\\", but with a rather dark funk edge. A lot of blippy sound effects - you can sense Orbit kicking into a higher gear. Madonna gets sultrier and lower, to fantastic results.<br /><br />\\"Skin\\" - a tense and unsettling uptempo piece. You can almost see the city chase scene in some sci-fi action movie. Plus, there are bagpipes or some similar foreign instrument. A highly intriguing track.<br /><br />\\"Nothing Really Matters\\" - Longtime Madonna producer Patrick Leonard returns, and it shows. Sounds remarkably similar to Madonna's 80's work in terms of composition, except it has instrumentation and sensibilities akin to the rest of the album.<br /><br />\\"Sky Fits Heaven\\" - A fascinating uptempo track about prophets, scriptures, and simplistic proverbs. Engaging and interesting.<br /><br />\\"Shanti/Ashtangi\\" - The eastern influences on the album come out full-force. Sounds middle-eastern or Indian, and probably draws significant influence from the trance subgenres that originated there, particularly Goa Trance (if you like this track, I recommend a psychedelic trance act named Cosmosis). The language Madonna is singing in is Sanskrit.<br /><br />\\"Frozen\\" - a chilling orchestral piece. Madonna is just as expressive as the moving strings that support her voice as she explores her lower register. A beautiful and haunting single that only gets better as Orbit brings in the shifting drum track.<br /><br />\\"The Power of Good-Bye\\" - riding \\"Frozen\\"'s wave, this song creates a downtempo current that makes the listener want to sway. The combination of the beat and the pulsating synths gives the track an almost snake-charming vibe.<br /><br />\\"To Have and Not to Hold\\" - an interesting break in the action. This song has latin influences that seem absent on the rest of the album, but it works in this setting. Very organic and engaging.<br /><br />\\"Little Star\\" - a lullaby to Madonna's daughter, Lourdes, and also an ode to self-expression. Has a charming quality. Madonna sounds as if she's about to burst into tears - you can sense her pain at the thought of the unavoidably public life that may await her child as she lives her life in her mother's shadow.<br /><br />\\"Mer Girl\\" - Quirky and brilliant. Madonna's surreal narrative in which she seems to morph back and forth (even in the same line) between herself as a little girl and herself as a confident mother. Cryptic in some ways but obvious in others. As it continues it becomes more and more unsettling but more and more fascinating. Writing this song may have been one of the most lucid introspective moments Madonna will ever have.<br /><br />This album is brilliant. $11 is an absolute bargain for music that is timeless and that you will never, ever forget or want to stop listening to.

B007L96SI6|3.0|As is often the case on American Idol, Haley Reinhart found herself on the wrong side of a momentum contest by the time the final three rolled around and she was voted off.  Regardless, throughout the competition, Reinhart garnered mixed reviews until finally things seemed to click and her full potential shined through.  That potential is tapped on the fine [[ASIN:B007L96SI6 Listen Up!]] which is nothing short of a surprise album materializing as a debut from an American Idol contestant.  That is not to say that Listen Up is the second coming (it's not), but it does find Haley opting in a more soulful, pop-soul direction, much like the soul she exhibited on her spirited take of \\"Bennie and The Jets\\" on the show.<br /><br />\\"Oh My,\\" featuring B.o.B opens Listen Up strongly with its old-school drum programming and vintage sounds. Thankfully, this production trend is one that graces the entirety of the album.  Reinhart's vocals are nuanced and incredibly soulful, layered in reverb much like classic soul. B.o.B's rap complements Reinhart's superb performance making \\"Oh My\\" that surprise, genuine Idol hit. \\"Free,\\" the promotional single, keeps things rolling and manages to contrast the retro-soul of \\"Oh My\\" with more of a classic-pop cut.  The live drums and the six-eight groove make \\"Free\\" a winner, not to mention Reinhart's commanding lead vocals and her lovely upper register.<br /><br />\\"Liar\\" has `big shoes to fill,' but presents yet another soulful cut that is not overt soul, but straddles pop and soul  Haley's lead vocals are as solid as ever and the background vocals again heighten the quality of the cut `up-a-notch.' \\"Liar\\" does, however, feel like it could use just `something' extra.  \\"Wasted Tears\\" is stronger, opening dramatic with a vintage-soul sound.  The harmonized background vocals continue to to dominate, supporting the quality lead of Reinhart.  The four-on-the-floor percussive groove truly channels retro soul, not to mention a fat bass line and horns.  The slight quibble might be that the ending seemed ever so slightly abrupt.  \\"Undone\\" shifts back from soul to pop, which is a smart move for the young pop starlet.  Minimal support by instruments at first allows Haley's voice to shine, adding more instruments to the refrain (predictably pop right?). Like many cuts on Listen Up, \\"Undone\\" is not without its flaws: the `big pop drums' fail to enter when you expect them to.  A little extra finesse would've put \\"Undone\\" over the top.<br /><br />\\"Now That You're Here\\" contrasts the balladry of \\"Undone\\" with a mid-tempo, four-on-the-floor soul-dance cut.  Intact are the dusty drums as well as a vintage-60s tambourine and EP.  \\"Wonderland\\" keeps the soulful, retro-soul stylings, though suffers a bit given the denseness of the cut.  At times, \\"Wonderland\\" feels a bit too cluttered with so many background vocals and a bit too much repetitiveness.<br /><br />\\"Keep Coming Back\\" restores any lost momentum, finding Reinhart achieving sound grit.  \\"Keep Coming Back\\" works better than most cuts on Listen Up because it straddles pop and soul without committing to either direction. The refrain is simple, but both catchy and effective.  \\"Hit The Ground Runnin'\\" keeps the momentum high, finding Reinhart among her most sassy.  The first verse is restrained allowing Haley to breathe, but by the refrain the production grows to full fruition sporting an assortment of instrumental timbres.  Busbee's production truly makes \\"Hit The Ground Runnin'\\" a strong cut for Haley.  Closing cut \\"Walking on Heaven\\" (also produced by busbee) features a nice funky groove and clean, clear production.  Reinhart's lead is commanding and the harmonic progressions nothing short of clever.  \\"Walking on Heaven\\" never reaches the top echelon, but closes a solid debut, well solidly.<br /><br />Overall [[ASIN:B007L96SI6 Listen Up!]] Is a well executed debut.  As stated, some finesse issues are to be found along the way with development of the song. That said, there is nothing that comes off as incredibly clumsy or filler material on this thin ten-track affair.re is nothing that comes off as incredibly clumsy or filler material on this thin ten-track affair.

B000002KZ6|4.0|When Talking Heads released \\"Speaking in Tongues,\\" that album followed the groove-heavy direction of its predecessors \\"Fear of Music\\" and \\"Remain in Light,\\" but took the dark, frantic music from the earlier album in a lighter, frothier direction.  Moreover, without Brian Eno's influence, the band stared drifting toward more pop-sounding songs and less intriguing subject matter.<br /><br />When I listen now to \\"Speaking in Tongues,\\" I feel that the band emphasis (or lead head Byrne anyway) also had shifted its emphasis from sound to visuals, from the music to the show, so that the visual aspect of what a performance of these songs would look like became more important than how the music sounded.<br /><br />That change in direction ultimately undermined the band, but the halo effect from the earlier two recordings was still strong enough so that \\"Speaking in Tongues\\" was a jaunty celebration, a churning, funky m&eacute;lange brimming with riffs, fills, percussion, and effects that worked well in this new context.<br /><br />\\"Stop Making Sense\\" followed, and that album was truly a soundtrack documenting the stage spectacle from the band's 1983-1984 tours. It was about the visuals, the stage show, the physical arrangement of musicians and Byrne's bit suit days. (I saw the band twice during this time, and the staging was unique and creative for its time.)<br /><br />But because of this change in emphasis, the music itself on to \\"Speaking in Tongues\\" suffers just a bit, and what were once muscular grooves and bizarre instrumental renderings were being transitioned to serve the melody and spectacle. Still, this was the last great Talking Heads' album (again, \\"Stop Making Sense\\" is a fine recording but it pales beside the video of the concert) before the band lost its direction on \\"Little Creatures\\" and \\"Naked.\\"

B007RXC3A8|4.0|Sonata Arctica will probably always hold on to their \\"power metal\\" classification, but their work has become more distinctive with each passing album and harder to lump together with more traditional power metal like Helloween or Blind Guardian. Their first albums (we'll call that era Phase I) gave metal fans a generous amount of satisfying tracks in that classic style, but hints of their future experimentation were always there. With Unia, the band made a sudden shift to their progressive era (we'll call it Phase II). Unia itself was a little rough, though not without some good stuff. The next album, The Days of Grays, was a large improvement and showed that the band's experimenting was yielding really interesting, satisfying results. At this point, you don't really know what you're going to get when you start listening to one of these albums, and there's definitely something exciting about that.<br /><br />In an album with a lot of experimenting, most of it succeeds and some of it doesn't. \\"Cinderblox\\" is a genre-busting masterpiece, combining country and metal to glorious effect. It's almost unthinkable that this could actually work but the final product speaks for itself. Who knew a banjo could synch up so well with double-bass drumming and keyboard solos? The funniest part is that aside from that banjo, this is the song on the album that comes closest to a classic Sonata track - the kind of song fans always say they miss. We're unlikely to get another \\"Wolf and Raven\\" or even another \\"Flag in the Ground,\\" those songs are already written. But if the band can still provide that same kind of thrill with an ingenious twist like this, I'll keep buying Sonata albums for decades.<br /><br />Other highlights include the goofy fun of \\"****load of Money,\\" a wacky combination of hair metal, Broadway theatricality and some of Queen's more flamboyant songs, the beautiful tearjerker \\"The Day,\\" the satisfyingly heavy \\"Somewhere Close to You\\" and \\"Wildfire,\\" parts II and III.These two tracks are complex, eight-minute prog monsters that continue the story of the unstable outcast that began in an excellent track on the Reckoning Night album. Both of these songs are such powerful storytelling that it not only invites comparison to the original \\"Wildfire\\" but also Sonata's ultimate masterpiece, \\"White Pearl, Black Oceans.\\" They blend everything that has been great about both Phase I and Phase II Sonata and I suspect the fans will love them. The story details, however, can get foggy. How exactly did we go from the angry pyromaniac of Part I to the bizarre environmental message whispered at the end of Part III?<br /><br />\\"Only the Broken Hearts (Make You Beautiful),\\" the first song, is not the most impressive opener. It starts off well enough, with a simple but memorable riff driving the intro. However, by the second verse the song has already been smothered by seemingly endless layers of harmonized vocals. \\"Don't Be Mean\\" is the kind of overwrought ballad about love gone wrong that the band can't get enough of. Musically, it's very pretty but the title and lyrics are a clear reminder that English isn't the band's first language. Hearing a grown man use the phrase \\"don't be mean to me\\" with such sincerity is a little cringe-inducing. As for the single, \\"I Have A Right,\\" it's not bad but it's as close to a straightforward pop song as the band's ever gotten. Fans of more aggressive sounding metal may be put off.<br /><br />Stones Grow Her Name is a strong album that the band can be proud of. I don't love every track, but the majority of them are great and even the lesser ones are still pleasant listens. As long as fans can accept that the straight power metal days of Sonata are long gone, this album will deliver.

B006M4RN3U|5.0|This CD gives me hope for the future of rock music. It's incredibly thrilling to hear a band that produces such strong, fresh, and interesting melodies and vocals that are as good as anything rock has produced since the 60s. The lyrics are perfectly fine, but the melodies and song structures are so strong that they seem to call for equally strong lyrics, and so by contrast they seem a little disappointing. What really makes this CD so compelling for me is the enthusiasm and energy they project and the singing, which is startling in its soaring range. The first time I heard We Are Young, it was almost as exciting as the first time I heard the Kink's You Really Got Me in 1964 (and to be clear, the musical sophistication here is leagues beyond the Kinks). My one complaint about the CD is the production, which is heavy handed and full of the usual heavily processed computerized noises, particularly in the bass and drums. For most of the music being made these days, this kind of heavy production is necessary, because there's no real melody to speak of, and singers, when they sing at all, are extremely weak. But this music doesn't need any props or cosmetics, and the production in this case actually gets in the way of the music. Next time, they need a producer who recognizes great songwriting and singing and who understands that production is supposed to bring out and support those things. But don't let that stop you from buying this. It is the freshest, best thing I've heard in many years, and the songs are completely stuck in my head, as they will be in yours.
0061692042|5.0|Every review of Canada is going to begin the same way, with the stunning opening sentences of the novel.  \\"First I'll tell you about the robbery our parents committed.  Then about the murders, which happened later.  The robbery is the more important part, since it served to set my and my sister's lives on the courses they eventually followed.\\"<br /><br />That's a bit more sensational than the average start of a serious literary work, but it telegraphs so much of what is to come.  In fact, I'll give you a run-down of what those opening sentences illustrate:<br /><br />* This novel is told from the point of view of a first-person narrator who speaks with a simple, clear voice.<br />* Despite the author's Pulitzer Prize-winning pedigree, this is a plot-driven novel bordering on a literary thriller.<br />* This is a coming-of-age tale.<br />* This novel is being told in reflection from some point in the future.<br /><br />That's a fair amount of info to glean from three sentences!<br /><br />The novel's narrator is 15-year-old Dell Parsons, one half of a set of fraternal twins.  The other half is his sister, Berner, older by six minutes and always the more worldly of the two.  The novel opens in the summer of 1960, and the family of four (with father, Bev and mother, Neeva) is living in Great Falls, Montana.  The kids have had a fairly rootless upbringing, due to Bev's Air Force career and a lack of extended family connections.<br /><br />Dell relates the family history, beginning with his parents' courtship and ill-advised marriage.  \\"...they were no doubt simply wrong for each other and should never have married or done any of it, should've gone their separate ways after their first passionate encounter, no matter its outcome.  The longer they stayed on, and the better they knew each other, the better she at least could see their mistake, and the more misguided their lives became as time went on--like a long proof in mathematics in which the first calculation is wrong, following which all other calculations move you further away from how things were when they made sense.\\"<br /><br />It's the older Dell, nearing retirement, that can look back on his past and family history and see things so clearly.  His story is told in a combination of his older and younger voices.  Nonetheless, given the above, it's no surprise he describes his family as \\"doomed.\\"  Bev doesn't adjust well to life outside the military, and a series of poor decisions leads the family, and particularly the teens, into dire and life-altering circumstances.<br /><br />Like all novels being told in reflection, this one features quite a bit of foreshadowing--again, you can see it in those opening sentences.  This continues throughout the novel, and there's a reason that foreshadowing is one of the most commonly used literary devices.  Because it's so darn effective!  Rather than diffusing the novel's tension, it ratchets it up, and it definitely keeps readers turning pages.  It's amazing how powerful a simple \\"I never saw her again\\" or \\"given how her life turned out\\" can be, and when the foreshadowing is of a crime, even more so.<br /><br />Despite the novel's page-turning plot, characters are given equal attention.  This is obvious early on as Dell describes his father, \\"He was a non-stop talker, was open-minded for a southerner, had graceful obliging manners that should've taken him far in the Air Force, but didn't.  His quick hazel eyes would search around any room he was in, finding someone to pay attention to him--my sister and me, ordinarily.  He told corny jokes in a southern theatrical style, could do card tricks and magic tricks, could detach his thumb and replace it, make a handkerchief disappear and come back.  He could play boogie-woogie piano, and sometimes would `talk Dixie' to us and sometimes like Amos `n' Andy.  He had lost some of his hearing by flying the Mitchells, and was sensitive about it.  But he looked sharp in his `honest' GI haircut and blue captain's tunic and generally conveyed a warmth that was genuine and made my twin sister and me love him.\\"  That's only a small part of Dell's recollection of Bev.  Could I describe my own father so well?  I doubt it.  Even relatively minor characters have a feel of completeness about them, leaving me with linger questions about them long after they'd come and gone.  How much did Mildred really know about her brother's life?  Did Florence see Dell again?<br /><br />The novel's prose is not ornate, but it's beautifully crafted.  Ford expertly paints the time and places in which the novel is set.  Clearly, I could go on quoting from and discussing this novel indefinitely, but better you should make these discoveries on your own.  Near the novel's end, Dell states, \\"There's little else to say.  I have that as my satisfaction.\\"  And by the time you reach this astonishing work's end, you'll have yours as well.e captain's tunic and generally conveyed a warmth that was genuine and made my twin sister and me love him.\\"  That's only a small part of Dell's recollection of Bev. The first stage, the one where you're falling and all dopey and basically unable to function because you can't think of anything but that other person is the result of a chemical cocktail of adrenaline, dopamine, and serotonin pouring through your brain. The second stage, the one where you're committed and stay together potentially for life is because of two other chemicals, vasopressin and oxytocin. These are the ones that really bond you together, but the fact is, once you're getting the vasopressin and oxytocin, you don't get so much of the adrenaline, dopamine, and serotonin. And that, my friends, is probably the biggest reason that I (and quite probably you) love to read about falling in love. It gives me a bit of those chemicals (and feelings) back. That's why after reading a book I love, I often can't sleep, and I usually have a lot of energy. And I love it!<br /><br />But here's the thing. 0312330537|4.0|Gregory David Roberts's Shantaram is supposed to be partly autobiographical, or at least loosely based on the author's own experience. That the novel is not entirely fictive is part of its appeal, veracity making its white-knuckle-ride of a plot all the more gripping. Still, be aware that Roberts himself has stated that the characters and plot details are imaginary, if grounded in real life.<br /><br />Shantaram relates the adventures of an escaped Australian convict, named Lin, in Bombay and its underworld. (Roberts himself escaped from an Australian prison and lived in India, and belonged to a Bombay mafia for a number of years.) Lin, having arrived almost by chance, soon grows roots in the great metropolis. He learns Hindi and Marathi, moves into a slum where he works as a doctor, befriends a migrant from a remote rural area, falls in love, saves a bear from prison, rescues an American girl from a notorious madame, and moves on to become a member of the city's most prominent gang, not all in that order. At some stage he is also jailed, and he goes to fight in Afghanistan for a spell (against the Soviets, as this takes place in the 1980s). The story is well told and the style of writing both agreeable and effective. Far from being written like a thriller, indeed, Shantaram has a distinct literary turn, with interesting observations and aphorisms. Perhaps its weakness is that it can be florid, and even preachy, at times. I would describe it as a cross between Paulo Coelho and Vikram Chandra. Anyway, this is well worth reading and is unlikely to disappoint.
